<span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Wakefield&amp;params=53.68_N_1.49_W_type:city_region:GB-WKF"><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">53°41′N</span> <span class="longitude">1°29′W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">&#xfeff; / &#xfeff;</span><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">53.68°N 1.49°W</span><span style="display:none">&#xfeff; / <span class="geo">53.68; -1.49</span></span></span></a></span><span style="font-size: small;"><span id="coordinates"><a href="/wiki/Geographic_coordinate_system" title="Geographic coordinate system">Coordinates</a>: <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Wakefield&amp;params=53.68_N_1.49_W_type:city_region:GB-WKF"><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">53°41′N</span> <span class="longitude">1°29′W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">&#xfeff; / &#xfeff;</span><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">53.68°N 1.49°W</span><span style="display:none">&#xfeff; / <span class="geo">53.68; -1.49</span></span></span></a></span></span></span></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Wakefield</b> is a cathedral city in <a href="/wiki/West_Yorkshire" title="West Yorkshire">West Yorkshire</a>, England, on the <a href="/wiki/River_Calder,_West_Yorkshire" title="River Calder, West Yorkshire">River Calder</a> and the eastern edge of the <a href="/wiki/Pennines" title="Pennines">Pennines</a>, which had a population of 99,251 at the 2011 census.<sup id="cite_ref-2011Census_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2011Census-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Wakefield" title="Battle of Wakefield">Battle of Wakefield</a> took place in the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Roses" title="Wars of the Roses">Wars of the Roses</a> and it was a <a href="/wiki/Cavalier" title="Cavalier">Royalist</a> stronghold in the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">Civil War</a>. Wakefield became an important <a href="/wiki/Market_town" title="Market town">market town</a> and centre for wool, exploiting its position on the navigable River Calder to become an <a href="/wiki/Inland_port" title="Inland port">inland port</a>.
</p><p>In the 18th&#160;century, Wakefield traded in corn, coal mining and textiles and in 1888 its parish church acquired <a href="/wiki/Cathedral" title="Cathedral">cathedral</a> status. It became the <a href="/wiki/County_town" title="County town">county town</a> of the West Riding of Yorkshire and was the seat of the <a href="/wiki/West_Riding_County_Council" title="West Riding County Council">West Riding County Council</a> from 1889 until 1974, when the county and council were abolished, and of the <a href="/wiki/West_Yorkshire" title="West Yorkshire">West Yorkshire</a> Metropolitan County Council from 1974 until its dissolution in 1986.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wakefield&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wakefield_-_Cathedral.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Wakefield_-_Cathedral.jpg/220px-Wakefield_-_Cathedral.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Wakefield_-_Cathedral.jpg/330px-Wakefield_-_Cathedral.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Wakefield_-_Cathedral.jpg/440px-Wakefield_-_Cathedral.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wakefield_-_Cathedral.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Wakefield_Cathedral" title="Wakefield Cathedral">Wakefield Cathedral</a>.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Toponymy">Toponymy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wakefield&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Toponymy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The name "Wakefield" may derive from "Waca's field" – the open land belonging to someone named "Waca" or could have evolved from the <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> word <i>wacu</i>, meaning "a watch or wake", and <i>feld</i>, an open field in which a wake or festival was held.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> In the <i><a href="/wiki/Domesday_Book" title="Domesday Book">Domesday Book</a></i> of 1086, it was written Wachefeld and also as Wachefelt.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history">Early history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wakefield&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Flint" title="Flint">Flint</a> and stone tools and later <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a> and iron implements have been found at Lee Moor and Lupset in the Wakefield area showing evidence of human activity since prehistoric times.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> This part of Yorkshire was home to the <a href="/wiki/Brigantes" title="Brigantes">Brigantes</a> until the Roman occupation in AD&#160;43. A Roman road from <a href="/wiki/Pontefract" title="Pontefract">Pontefract</a> passing Streethouse, Heath Common, Ossett Street Side, through <a href="/wiki/Kirklees" title="Kirklees">Kirklees</a> and on to <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a> crossed the <a href="/wiki/River_Calder,_West_Yorkshire" title="River Calder, West Yorkshire">River Calder</a> by a ford at Wakefield near the site of Wakefield Bridge.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Wakefield was probably settled by the <a href="/wiki/Angles" title="Angles">Angles</a> in the 5th or 6th&#160;century and after AD&#160;876 the area was controlled by the <a href="/wiki/Vikings" title="Vikings">Vikings</a> who founded twelve hamlets or <i>thorpes</i> around Wakefield.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;nb 1&#93;</a></sup> They divided the area into <a href="/wiki/Wapentakes" class="mw-redirect" title="Wapentakes">wapentakes</a> and Wakefield was part of the <a href="/wiki/Agbrigg_and_Morley" title="Agbrigg and Morley">Wapentake of Agbrigg</a>. The settlement grew near a crossing place on the River Calder around three roads, Westgate, Northgate and Kirkgate.<sup id="cite_ref-Historic_Wakefield_EarlyHistory_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Historic_Wakefield_EarlyHistory-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The "gate" suffix derives from Old Norse <i><i lang="non" title="Old Norse language text">gata</i></i> meaning road<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> and kirk, from <i><i lang="non" title="Old Norse language text">kirkja</i></i> indicates there was a church.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Before 1066 the <a href="/wiki/Manor" title="Manor">manor</a> of Wakefield belonged to <a href="/wiki/Edward_the_Confessor" title="Edward the Confessor">Edward the Confessor</a> and it passed to <a href="/wiki/William_the_Conqueror" title="William the Conqueror">William the Conqueror</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hastings" title="Battle of Hastings">Battle of Hastings</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_England" title="Norman conquest of England">Conquest</a> Wakefield was a victim of the <a href="/wiki/Harrying_of_the_North" title="Harrying of the North">Harrying of the North</a> in 1069 when William the Conqueror took revenge on the local population for resistance to Norman rule. The settlement was recorded as <i>Wachfeld</i> in the <a href="/wiki/Domesday_Book" title="Domesday Book">Domesday Book</a> of 1086, and covered a much greater area than present day Wakefield, much of which was described as "waste".<sup id="cite_ref-Manor_of_Wakefield_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Manor_of_Wakefield-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The manor was granted by <a href="/wiki/The_Crown" title="The Crown">the crown</a> to <a href="/wiki/William_de_Warenne,_1st_Earl_of_Surrey" title="William de Warenne, 1st Earl of Surrey">William de Warenne, 1st Earl of Surrey</a> whose descendants, the Earls Warenne, inherited it after his death in 1088.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The construction of <a href="/wiki/Sandal_Castle" title="Sandal Castle">Sandal Castle</a> began early in the 12th&#160;century.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> A second castle was built at <a href="/wiki/Wakefield_Castle" title="Wakefield Castle">Lawe Hill</a> on the north side of the Calder but was abandoned.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Wakefield and its environs formed the <a href="/wiki/Caput_baroniae" title="Caput baroniae">caput</a> of an extensive baronial holding by the Warennes that extended to <a href="/wiki/Cheshire" title="Cheshire">Cheshire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lancashire" title="Lancashire">Lancashire</a>. The Warennes, and their <a href="/wiki/Feudal" class="mw-redirect" title="Feudal">feudal</a> sublords, held the area until the 14th&#160;century, when it passed to their heirs.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
Norman tenants holding land in the region included the <a href="/wiki/Levett" title="Levett">Lyvet</a> family at Lupset.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Domesday Book recorded two churches, one in Wakefield and one in <a href="/wiki/Sandal_Magna" title="Sandal Magna">Sandal Magna</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The Saxon church in Wakefield was rebuilt in about 1100 in stone in the <a href="/wiki/Norman_architecture" title="Norman architecture">Norman</a> style and was continually enlarged until 1315 when the central tower collapsed. By 1420 the church was again rebuilt and was extended between 1458 and 1475.
</p><p>In 1203 <a href="/wiki/William_de_Warenne,_5th_Earl_of_Surrey" title="William de Warenne, 5th Earl of Surrey">William de Warenne, 5th Earl of Surrey</a> received a grant for a market in the town.<sup id="cite_ref-Historic_Wakefield_1066-1600_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Historic_Wakefield_1066-1600-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> In 1204 <a href="/wiki/John,_King_of_England" title="John, King of England">King John</a> granted the rights for a fair at the feast of <a href="/wiki/All_Saints%27_Day" title="All Saints&#39; Day">All Saints</a>, 1 November, and in 1258 <a href="/wiki/Henry_III_of_England" title="Henry III of England">Henry III</a> granted the right for fair on the feast of Saint John the Baptist, 24 June. The market was close to the Bull Ring and the church.<sup id="cite_ref-Historic_Wakefield_1066-1600_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Historic_Wakefield_1066-1600-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The townsfolk of Wakefield amused themselves in games and sports earning the title "Merrie Wakefield", the chief sport in the 14th&#160;century was <a href="/wiki/Archery" title="Archery">archery</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Archery_butts" class="mw-redirect" title="Archery butts">butts</a> in Wakefield were at the <a href="/wiki/Ings" title="Ings">Ings</a>, near the river.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Richard_of_York_Memorial_-_geograph.org.uk_-_858000.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Richard_of_York_Memorial_-_geograph.org.uk_-_858000.jpg/120px-Richard_of_York_Memorial_-_geograph.org.uk_-_858000.jpg" decoding="async" width="120" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Richard_of_York_Memorial_-_geograph.org.uk_-_858000.jpg/180px-Richard_of_York_Memorial_-_geograph.org.uk_-_858000.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Richard_of_York_Memorial_-_geograph.org.uk_-_858000.jpg/240px-Richard_of_York_Memorial_-_geograph.org.uk_-_858000.jpg 2x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="640" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Richard_of_York_Memorial_-_geograph.org.uk_-_858000.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Memorial to the <a href="/wiki/Richard_of_York,_3rd_Duke_of_York" title="Richard of York, 3rd Duke of York">Duke of York</a>, killed in battle, 1460</div></div></div>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Roses" title="Wars of the Roses">Wars of the Roses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Plantagenet,_Duke_of_York" class="mw-redirect" title="Richard Plantagenet, Duke of York">Richard Plantagenet, Duke of York</a> was killed on 30 December 1460 in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Wakefield" title="Battle of Wakefield">Battle of Wakefield</a> near Sandal Castle. As preparation for the impending invasion by the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Armada" title="Spanish Armada">Spanish Armada</a> in April 1558, 400 men from the wapentake of Morley and Agbrigg were summoned to Bruntcliffe near <a href="/wiki/Morley,_West_Yorkshire" title="Morley, West Yorkshire">Morley</a> with their weapons. Men from Kirkgate, Westgate, Northgate and Sandal were amongst them and all returned by August.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> At the time of the Civil War, Wakefield was a <a href="/wiki/Cavalier" title="Cavalier">Royalist</a> stronghold. An attack led by Sir <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Fairfax" title="Thomas Fairfax">Thomas Fairfax</a> on 20 May 1643 captured the town for the <a href="/wiki/Roundhead" title="Roundhead">Parliamentarians</a>. Over 1500 troops were taken prisoner along with the Royalist commander, <a href="/wiki/George_Goring,_Lord_Goring" title="George Goring, Lord Goring">Lieutenant-General Goring</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-British_Civil_Wars_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-British_Civil_Wars-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Medieval" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval">medieval</a> times Wakefield became an inland port on the Calder and centre for the woollen and <a href="/wiki/Tanning_(leather)" title="Tanning (leather)">tanning</a> trades. In 1699 an Act of Parliament was passed creating the <a href="/wiki/Aire_and_Calder_Navigation" title="Aire and Calder Navigation">Aire and Calder Navigation</a> which provided the town with access to the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Historic_Wakefield_1600-1800_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Historic_Wakefield_1600-1800-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The first Registry of Deeds in the country opened in 1704 and in 1765 Wakefield's cattle market was established and became the one of largest in the north of England. The town was a centre for cloth dealing, with its own piece hall, the Tammy Hall, built in 1766.<sup id="cite_ref-Old_Wakefield_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Old_Wakefield-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> In the late 1700s <a href="/wiki/Georgian_architecture" title="Georgian architecture">Georgian</a> town houses and St John's Church were built to the north of the town centre.<sup id="cite_ref-Historic_Wakefield_1600-1800_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Historic_Wakefield_1600-1800-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-St_Johns_Church/_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-St_Johns_Church/-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Wakefield was dubbed the "Merrie City" in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> and in 1538 <a href="/wiki/John_Leland_(antiquary)" title="John Leland (antiquary)">John Leland</a> described it as, "a very quick market town and meately large; well served of fish and flesh both from sea and by rivers ... so that all vitaile is very good and chepe there. A right honest man shall fare well for 2d. a meal. ... There be plenti of se coal in the quarters about Wakefield".<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;nb 2&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_Revolution">Industrial Revolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wakefield&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Industrial Revolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wakefield_Westgate.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Wakefield_Westgate.jpg/220px-Wakefield_Westgate.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Wakefield_Westgate.jpg/330px-Wakefield_Westgate.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Wakefield_Westgate.jpg/440px-Wakefield_Westgate.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1622" data-file-height="1039" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wakefield_Westgate.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wakefield Westgate <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;1900</span></div></div></div>
<p>At the start of 19th&#160;century Wakefield was a wealthy market town and inland port trading in wool and grain.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Aire_and_Calder_Navigation" title="Aire and Calder Navigation">Aire and Calder</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calder_and_Hebble_Navigation" title="Calder and Hebble Navigation">Calder and Hebble Navigations</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Barnsley_Canal" title="Barnsley Canal">Barnsley Canal</a> were instrumental in the development of Wakefield as an important market for grain and more was sold here than at any other market in the north. Large warehouses were built on the river banks to store grain from <a href="/wiki/Norfolk" title="Norfolk">Norfolk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cambridgeshire" title="Cambridgeshire">Cambridgeshire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lincolnshire" title="Lincolnshire">Lincolnshire</a> to supply the fast-growing population in the <a href="/wiki/West_Riding_of_Yorkshire" title="West Riding of Yorkshire">West Riding of Yorkshire</a>. Great quantities of barley were grown in the neighbourhood and in 1885 more <a href="/wiki/Malt" title="Malt">malt</a> was made in Wakefield "than in any district of equal extent in the kingdom".<sup id="cite_ref-TDE_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TDE-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The market developed in the streets around the Bull Ring, and the cattle market between George Street and Ings Road grew to be one of the biggest in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Road transport using <a href="/wiki/Turnpike_trust" class="mw-redirect" title="Turnpike trust">turnpiked</a> roads was important. Regular <a href="/wiki/Mail_coach" title="Mail coach">mail coaches</a> departed to <a href="/wiki/Leeds" title="Leeds">Leeds</a>, <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, Manchester, <a href="/wiki/York" title="York">York</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sheffield" title="Sheffield">Sheffield</a> and the 'Strafford Arms' was an important <a href="/wiki/Coaching_inn" title="Coaching inn">coaching inn</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The railways arrived in Wakefield in 1840 when <a href="/wiki/Wakefield_Kirkgate_railway_station" title="Wakefield Kirkgate railway station">Kirkgate station</a> was built on the <a href="/wiki/Manchester_and_Leeds_Railway" title="Manchester and Leeds Railway">Manchester and Leeds Railway</a>.
</p><p>When cloth dealing declined, wool spinning mills using steam power were built by the river. There was a glass works in Calder Vale Road, several <a href="/wiki/Brewery" title="Brewery">breweries</a> including Melbourne's and Beverley's Eagle Breweries, engineering works with strong links to the mining industry, soapworks and brickyards in Eastmoor, giving the town a diverse economy.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Historic_Wakefield_1800-1900_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Historic_Wakefield_1800-1900-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Boats and <a href="/wiki/Sloop" title="Sloop">sloops</a> were built at yards on the Calder.<sup id="cite_ref-TDE_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TDE-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> On the outskirts of the town, coal had been dug since the 15th&#160;century and 300 men were employed in the town's coal pits in 1831.<sup id="cite_ref-Old_Wakefield_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Old_Wakefield-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> During the 19th&#160;century more mines were sunk so that there were 46&#160;small mines in Wakefield and the surrounding area by 1869.<sup id="cite_ref-Historic_Wakefield_1800-1900_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Historic_Wakefield_1800-1900-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/National_Coal_Board" title="National Coal Board">National Coal Board</a> eventually became Wakefield's largest employer with Manor Colliery on Cross Lane and Park Hill colliery at Eastmoor surviving until 1982.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the 19th&#160;century Wakefield became the administrative centre for the <a href="/wiki/West_Riding_of_Yorkshire" title="West Riding of Yorkshire">West Riding</a>, when many familiar buildings were constructed.<sup id="cite_ref-Taylor_2008_43_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor_2008_43-36">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> The first civic building in Wood Street, the court house, was built in 1810.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Royd_Hospital" title="Stanley Royd Hospital">West Riding Pauper Lunatic Asylum</a> was built at Stanley Royd, just outside the town on Aberford Road in 1816. During the 19th&#160;century, the Wakefield Asylum played a central role in the development of British psychiatry, with <a href="/wiki/Henry_Maudsley" title="Henry Maudsley">Henry Maudsley</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_Crichton-Browne" title="James Crichton-Browne">James Crichton-Browne</a> amongst its medical staff. Most of it is now demolished. The old House of Correction of 1595 was rebuilt as <a href="/wiki/Wakefield_Prison" class="mw-redirect" title="Wakefield Prison">Wakefield Prison</a> in 1847.<sup id="cite_ref-Prison_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prison-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Wakefield Union workhouse<sup id="cite_ref-workhouses_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-workhouses-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> was built on Park Lodge Lane, Eastmoor in 1853 and Clayton Hospital was built in 1854 after a donation from Alderman Thomas Clayton.<sup id="cite_ref-Taylor_2008_43_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor_2008_43-36">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> The Mechanics Institute containing an Assembly Room, public library and newsroom supported by subscription was built in Wood Street in 1820-1821 in the <a href="/wiki/Classical_style" class="mw-redirect" title="Classical style">Classical style</a> with <a href="/wiki/Ionic_order" title="Ionic order">Ionic</a> details. Wakefield Literary Society ran there from 1827 until the 20th&#160;century and its Geological Society left artefacts to Wakefield Museum.<sup id="cite_ref-TDE_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TDE-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Up to 1837 Wakefield relied on wells and springs for its water supply; water from the River Calder was polluted, and various water supply schemes were unsuccessful until <a href="/wiki/Reservoir" title="Reservoir">reservoirs</a> on the Rishworth Moors and a service reservoir at Ardsley were built providing clean water from 1888.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
By 1885 the streets of the town were paved and flagged and lit with gas supplied by a company incorporated in 1822.<sup id="cite_ref-TDE_29-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TDE-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Between 1870-1885 improvements were made on the north side of town around St John's Church now a <a href="/wiki/Conservation_area" class="mw-redirect" title="Conservation area">conservation area</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wakefield&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On 2 June 1906, <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Carnegie" title="Andrew Carnegie">Andrew Carnegie</a> opened a new Wakefield Library on Drury Lane which had been built with a grant of £8,000 from the Carnegie Trust.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There are seven <a href="/wiki/Council_house" title="Council house">ex-council estates</a> in Wakefield which the council started to build after the <a href="/wiki/First_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First World War">First World War</a>, the oldest is Portobello, the largest is Lupset and the rest are <a href="/wiki/Flanshaw" title="Flanshaw">Flanshaw</a>, Plumpton, Peacock, Eastmoor and <a href="/wiki/Kettlethorpe,_West_Yorkshire" title="Kettlethorpe, West Yorkshire">Kettlethorpe</a>. Homes not bought by occupants under the <a href="/wiki/Right_to_Buy" title="Right to Buy">Right to Buy</a> scheme were transferred to a registered social landlord, Wakefield and District Housing (WDH) in 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-WDH_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WDH-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The outlying villages of <a href="/wiki/Sandal_Magna" title="Sandal Magna">Sandal Magna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belle_Vue_(Wakefield)" title="Belle Vue (Wakefield)">Belle Vue</a> and <a href="/wiki/Agbrigg" title="Agbrigg">Agbrigg</a> have become suburbs of Wakefield.
</p><p>The glass and textile industries closed in the 1970s and 1980s, and coal faced competition from alternative sources and demand decreased.  The coal mines around Wakefield were amongst the first in Yorkshire to close under the government of <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a>, which altered the national energy policy from a reliance on British coal and opposed the political power of the <a href="/wiki/National_Union_of_Mineworkers_(Great_Britain)" title="National Union of Mineworkers (Great Britain)">NUM</a>. Between 1979 and 1983, the pits at Lofthouse, Manor, Newmarket, Newmillerdam, Parkhill and Walton all closed.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> As the Wakefield pits closed, the <a href="/wiki/Selby_Coalfield" title="Selby Coalfield">Selby Coalfield</a> was being opened, many colliers in Wakefield accepted offers to transfer to the new pits which were built to facilitate commuting.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Governance">Governance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wakefield&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Governance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_(new)_Town_Hall,_Wood_Street,_Wakefield.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/The_%28new%29_Town_Hall%2C_Wood_Street%2C_Wakefield.jpg/150px-The_%28new%29_Town_Hall%2C_Wood_Street%2C_Wakefield.jpg" decoding="async" width="150" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/The_%28new%29_Town_Hall%2C_Wood_Street%2C_Wakefield.jpg/225px-The_%28new%29_Town_Hall%2C_Wood_Street%2C_Wakefield.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/The_%28new%29_Town_Hall%2C_Wood_Street%2C_Wakefield.jpg/300px-The_%28new%29_Town_Hall%2C_Wood_Street%2C_Wakefield.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1712" data-file-height="2288" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_(new)_Town_Hall,_Wood_Street,_Wakefield.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wakefield Town Hall</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wakefield_County_Hall.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wakefield_County_Hall.jpg/150px-Wakefield_County_Hall.jpg" decoding="async" width="150" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wakefield_County_Hall.jpg/225px-Wakefield_County_Hall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wakefield_County_Hall.jpg/300px-Wakefield_County_Hall.jpg 2x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="640" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wakefield_County_Hall.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wakefield County Hall</div></div></div>
<p>Wakefield was anciently a market and parish town in the Agbrigg division of the <a href="/wiki/Wapentake" class="mw-redirect" title="Wapentake">wapentake</a> of <a href="/wiki/Agbrigg_and_Morley" title="Agbrigg and Morley">Agbrigg and Morley</a> in the <a href="/wiki/West_Riding_of_Yorkshire" title="West Riding of Yorkshire">West Riding of Yorkshire</a>. It became a parliamentary borough with one Member of Parliament after the <a href="/wiki/Reform_Act_1832" title="Reform Act 1832">Reform Act 1832</a>. In 1836 the Wakefield <a href="/wiki/Poor_Law_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Poor Law Union">Poor Law Union</a> was formed following the <a href="/wiki/Poor_Law_Amendment_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Poor Law Amendment Act">Poor Law Amendment Act</a> 1834 with an elected Board of Guardians.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> The town was incorporated as a <a href="/wiki/Municipal_borough" title="Municipal borough">municipal borough</a> with elected councillors in 1848 under the Municipal Corporations Act 1835.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Wakefield was the de facto seat of regional government in Yorkshire for two centuries and became the county headquarters of the West Riding County Council created by the <a href="/wiki/Local_Government_Act_of_1888" class="mw-redirect" title="Local Government Act of 1888">Local Government Act of 1888</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> After Wakefield was elevated to diocese in 1888, Wakefield Council sought city status which was granted the same year.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Wakefield became a <a href="/wiki/County_borough" title="County borough">county borough</a> in 1913.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> In 1974, under the terms of the <a href="/wiki/Local_Government_Act_1972" title="Local Government Act 1972">Local Government Act 1972</a>, the county borough became defunct as it merged with surrounding local authorities to become the <a href="/wiki/City_of_Wakefield" title="City of Wakefield">City of Wakefield</a> district.
</p><p>Today the city is the headquarters of <a href="/wiki/Wakefield_Metropolitan_District_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="Wakefield Metropolitan District Council">Wakefield Metropolitan District Council</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire_Ambulance_Service" title="Yorkshire Ambulance Service">Yorkshire Ambulance Service</a> and <a href="/wiki/West_Yorkshire_Police" title="West Yorkshire Police">West Yorkshire Police</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AboutWakefield_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AboutWakefield-51">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-lgyh_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lgyh-52">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Since 1987, the district council has been based in <a href="/wiki/County_Hall,_Wakefield" title="County Hall, Wakefield">County Hall</a>.
</p><p>Wakefield is covered by four electoral wards (Wakefield East, Wakefield North, Wakefield South and Wakefield West) of the Wakefield Metropolitan District Council. Each ward elects three councillors to the 63-member metropolitan district council, Wakefield's local authority. In 2015 all the councillors elected for Wakefield East, North and West were members of the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> and the councillors for Wakefield South represent the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative Party</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Wakefield_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Wakefield (UK Parliament constituency)">Wakefield</a>'s MP is <a href="/wiki/Mary_Creagh" title="Mary Creagh">Mary Creagh</a> who has represented the parliamentary seat for the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> since the 2005 <a href="/wiki/General_Election" class="mw-redirect" title="General Election">General Election</a>. She was re-elected in 2010 with a reduced majority<sup id="cite_ref-2010_Election_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2010_Election-54">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> and returned with a 40.3 share of the vote in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-Election_2015_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Election_2015-55">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> The seat has been held by the Labour Party since 1932. The Wakefield South ward covering Sandal, Kettlethorpe, Agbrigg and Belle Vue, is in the <a href="/wiki/Hemsworth_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Hemsworth (UK Parliament constituency)">Hemsworth constituency</a>, represented by the Labour party's <a href="/wiki/Jon_Trickett" title="Jon Trickett">Jon Trickett</a> since 1996. He was re-elected in May 2010<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> and returned in 2015 with 51.3% of the vote.<sup id="cite_ref-Hemsworth_2015_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hemsworth_2015-57">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> The seat has been held by the Labour Party since its creation in 1918.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wakefield&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Wakefield is 9 miles (14&#160;km) south-east of Leeds and 28 miles (45&#160;km) southwest of York on the eastern edge of the <a href="/wiki/Pennines" title="Pennines">Pennines</a> in the lower Calder Valley. The city centre is sited on a low hill on the north bank of the Calder close to a crossing place where it is spanned by a 14th-century, nine-arched, stone bridge and a reinforced concrete bridge built in 1929–1930.<sup id="cite_ref-Wakefield_Bridge_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wakefield_Bridge-58">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wakefield_New_Bridge_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wakefield_New_Bridge-59">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> It is at the junction of major north-south routes to Sheffield, Leeds and Doncaster and west-east routes to Huddersfield, Dewsbury and Pontefract.
</p><p>Wakefield is within the area of the Nottinghamshire, Derbyshire &amp; Yorkshire coalfield and lies on the middle <a href="/wiki/Coal_measure" class="mw-redirect" title="Coal measure">coal measures</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstones</a> laid down in the <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a> period.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Wakefield includes the former outlying villages of <a href="/wiki/Alverthorpe" title="Alverthorpe">Alverthorpe</a>, Thornes, Sandal Magna, Agbrigg, Lupset, Kettlethorpe and Flanshaw.  In the 2011 Census, Newton Hill, Outwood, Stanley and Wrenthorpe were counted as parts of Wakefield, having been classified separately in the 2001 Census.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demography">Demography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wakefield&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Demography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="wikitable" id="toc" style="float:right; margin-left: 1em; width: 40%; font-size: 90%;" cellspacing="5">

<tbody><tr>
<th colspan="4"><b>Wakefield Compared in 2008</b>
</th></tr>

<p>In 2001 the Wakefield urban area had a population of 76,886<sup id="cite_ref-KS01_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KS01-63">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> comprising 37,477 males and 39,409 females.<sup id="cite_ref-KS01_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KS01-63">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
Also at the time of the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Census_2001" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom Census 2001">2001 UK census</a>, the <a href="/wiki/City_of_Wakefield" title="City of Wakefield">City of Wakefield</a> had a total population of 315,172 of whom 161,962 were female and 153,210 were male. Of the 132,212 households in Wakefield, 39.56% were married couples living together, 28.32% were one-person households, 9.38% were <a href="/wiki/Cohabitation" title="Cohabitation">co-habiting</a> couples and 9.71% were lone parents. The figures for lone parent households were slightly above the national average of 9.5%, and the percentage of married couples was above the national average of 36.5%; the proportion of one person households was below the national average of 30.1%.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The population density was 9.31/km<sup>2</sup> (24.1/sq&#160;mi). Of those aged 16–74 in Wakefield, 39.14% had no <a href="/wiki/National_Qualifications_Framework" class="mw-redirect" title="National Qualifications Framework">academic qualifications</a>, much higher than 28.9% in all of England. Of Wakefield's residents, 2.53% were born outside the United Kingdom, significantly lower than the national average of 9.2%. The largest minority group was recorded as Asian, at 1.41% of the population.
</p><p>The number of theft-from-a-vehicle offences and theft of a vehicle per 1,000 of the population was 7.9 and 3.9 compared to the English national average of 6.3 and 2.3 respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-CrimeFigs_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CrimeFigs-65">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> The number of sexual offences was 0.9, in line with the national average.<sup id="cite_ref-CrimeFigs_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CrimeFigs-65">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> The national average of violence against another person was 16.7 compared to the Wakefield average of 15.<sup id="cite_ref-CrimeFigs_65-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CrimeFigs-65">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> The figures for crime statistics were all recorded during the 2008&#8211;09 financial year.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Population_change">Population change</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wakefield&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Population change">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Wakefield had a population of 76,886 in 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-Census2001tableKS01_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Census2001tableKS01-66">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>   At the 2011 Census, the population was given as 99,251.<sup id="cite_ref-2011Census_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2011Census-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>  However, these two estimates are not directly comparable, as the 2001 Census did not classify the old <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Urban_District,_Yorkshire" class="mw-redirect" title="Stanley Urban District, Yorkshire">Stanley Urban District</a> as part of Wakefield whereas the 2011 Census classified all settlements in this area except Bottom Boat (a small village built for Newmarket Colliery workers) as parts of Wakefield.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wakefield&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="wikitable" id="toc" style="float: right; margin-left: 1em; width: 40%; font-size: 90%; text-align:center" cellspacing="5">

<tbody><tr>
<th colspan="5"><b>Wakefield Compared</b>

</th></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The economy of Wakefield declined in the last quarter of the 20th&#160;century as the coal mines and traditional manufacturing industries closed, contributing to high rates of unemployment. In the <a href="/wiki/Index_of_Multiple_Deprivation" class="mw-redirect" title="Index of Multiple Deprivation">Index of Multiple Deprivation</a> 2015, 14.35% of the district's lower super-output areas were in the most deprived 10% of England.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Employment grew by 12% between 1998 and 2003 as the economy recovered and enjoyed growth as the economic base of the district was diversified. Growth has been supported by inward investment from European and United Kingdom government funding which has impacted on the regeneration of the area. Manufacturing remains an important employment sector although the decline is projected to continue whilst distribution and the service industries are now among the main employers.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At the 2001 census, there were 33,521 people in employment who were resident within Wakefield. Of these, 20.74% worked in the wholesale and retail trade, including repair of motor vehicles; 14.42% worked within manufacturing industry; 11% worked within the health and social work sector and 6.49% were employed in the transport, storage and communication industries.<sup id="cite_ref-2001industrydata_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2001industrydata-72">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
Wakefield is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Leeds_City_Region_Partnership" class="mw-redirect" title="Leeds City Region Partnership">Leeds City Region Partnership</a>, a sub-regional economic development partnership covering an area of the historic county of Yorkshire.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regeneration">Regeneration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wakefield&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Regeneration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Trinity_Walk,_Wakefield_(10th_March_2013)_003.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Trinity_Walk%2C_Wakefield_%2810th_March_2013%29_003.JPG/220px-Trinity_Walk%2C_Wakefield_%2810th_March_2013%29_003.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Trinity_Walk%2C_Wakefield_%2810th_March_2013%29_003.JPG/330px-Trinity_Walk%2C_Wakefield_%2810th_March_2013%29_003.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Trinity_Walk%2C_Wakefield_%2810th_March_2013%29_003.JPG/440px-Trinity_Walk%2C_Wakefield_%2810th_March_2013%29_003.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3872" data-file-height="2176" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Trinity_Walk,_Wakefield_(10th_March_2013)_003.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Trinity Walk shopping centre</div></div></div>
<p>Regeneration projects in Wakefield included the <a href="/wiki/Trinity_Walk" title="Trinity Walk">Trinity Walk</a> retail development to the north east of the city centre, including department stores, a supermarket and shop units.<sup id="cite_ref-Major_Development_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Major_Development-74">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Work began in autumn 2007 but was halted in 2009, restarted in 2010 and opened in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-Trinity_Walk_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Trinity_Walk-75">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The central square at the Bull Ring has been redesigned with a water feature and the Ridings Shopping Centre refurbished.<sup id="cite_ref-Bull_Ring_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bull_Ring-76">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>  Wakefield Westgate Station goods yard and land on Westgate and Balne Lane have been developed to create retail, residential and commercial space including new offices, a multi-storey carpark serving the station, and a hotel.<sup id="cite_ref-Westgate_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Westgate-77">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Developments by the river and canal, the "Wakefield Waterfront", include the refurbishment of the Grade II listed Navigation Warehouse and office, retail, restaurant and cafe units. The development includes the art gallery, <a href="/wiki/The_Hepworth_Wakefield" title="The Hepworth Wakefield">The Hepworth Wakefield</a> named in honour of local sculptor, <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Hepworth" title="Barbara Hepworth">Barbara Hepworth</a> which opened in May 2011. The gallery has ten internal spaces, exhibiting many examples of Hepworth's work. The gallery added about £10&#160;million to the local economy by attracting 500,000 visitors in its first year.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Flats and offices were built at Chantry Waters, on an island between the river and canal.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Landmarks">Landmarks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wakefield&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Landmarks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chantry_Bridge.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Chantry_Bridge.JPG/220px-Chantry_Bridge.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Chantry_Bridge.JPG/330px-Chantry_Bridge.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Chantry_Bridge.JPG/440px-Chantry_Bridge.JPG 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chantry_Bridge.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chantry Bridge over the <a href="/wiki/River_Calder,_West_Yorkshire" title="River Calder, West Yorkshire">River Calder</a></div></div></div>
<p>The most prominent landmark in Wakefield is <a href="/wiki/Wakefield_Cathedral" title="Wakefield Cathedral">Wakefield Cathedral</a>, which at 247 feet (75&#160;m) has the tallest spire in Yorkshire.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Other landmarks in the Civic Quarter on Wood Street include the Grade II* <a href="/wiki/Neoclassical_architecture" title="Neoclassical architecture">Neoclassical</a> Crown Court of 1810,<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Wakefield Town Hall designed by T.W. Collcutt and opened in 1880,<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Queen_Anne_Style_architecture" class="mw-redirect" title="Queen Anne Style architecture">Queen Anne Style</a> County Hall of 1898 which are Grade I listed.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> St John's Church and Square, St John's North and South Parade are part of residential development dating from the Georgian period.
</p><p>The old Wakefield Bridge with its Chantry Chapel, Sandal Castle, and Lawe Hill in Clarence Park are <a href="/wiki/Scheduled_monument" title="Scheduled monument">ancient monuments</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
Another prominent structure is the 95-arch railway viaduct, constructed of 800,000,000 bricks in the 1860s on the Doncaster to Leeds railway line. At its northern end is a bridge with an 80-foot (24&#160;m) span over Westgate and at its southern end a 163-foot (50&#160;m) iron bridge crossing the River Calder.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport">Transport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wakefield&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Transport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Railway_Viaduct_-_geograph.org.uk_-_362217.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Railway_Viaduct_-_geograph.org.uk_-_362217.jpg/220px-Railway_Viaduct_-_geograph.org.uk_-_362217.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Railway_Viaduct_-_geograph.org.uk_-_362217.jpg/330px-Railway_Viaduct_-_geograph.org.uk_-_362217.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Railway_Viaduct_-_geograph.org.uk_-_362217.jpg/440px-Railway_Viaduct_-_geograph.org.uk_-_362217.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="421" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Railway_Viaduct_-_geograph.org.uk_-_362217.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The brick-built 95-arch viaduct in Wakefield</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:23_Wakefield_Kirkgate_Railway_Station_(flash)_-wikipedia-.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/23_Wakefield_Kirkgate_Railway_Station_%28flash%29_-wikipedia-.jpg/220px-23_Wakefield_Kirkgate_Railway_Station_%28flash%29_-wikipedia-.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/23_Wakefield_Kirkgate_Railway_Station_%28flash%29_-wikipedia-.jpg/330px-23_Wakefield_Kirkgate_Railway_Station_%28flash%29_-wikipedia-.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/23_Wakefield_Kirkgate_Railway_Station_%28flash%29_-wikipedia-.jpg/440px-23_Wakefield_Kirkgate_Railway_Station_%28flash%29_-wikipedia-.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="726" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:23_Wakefield_Kirkgate_Railway_Station_(flash)_-wikipedia-.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Wakefield_Kirkgate_railway_station" title="Wakefield Kirkgate railway station">Wakefield Kirkgate railway station</a></div></div></div>
<p>Wakefield has good access to the motorway system, with the intersection of the <a href="/wiki/M1_motorway" title="M1 motorway">M1</a> and <a href="/wiki/M62_motorway" title="M62 motorway">M62</a> motorways, junctions 42/29, lying to the north west, whilst the M1 to the west is accessed at junctions 39, 40 and 41. The <a href="/wiki/A1(M)_motorway_(Great_Britain)" class="mw-redirect" title="A1(M) motorway (Great Britain)">A1(M)</a> is to the east of the district. Wakefield is crossed by the <a href="/wiki/A61_road" title="A61 road">A61</a>, <a href="/wiki/A638_road" title="A638 road">A638</a>, and <a href="/wiki/A642_road" title="A642 road">A642</a> roads and is the starting point of the <a href="/wiki/A636_road" title="A636 road">A636</a> and <a href="/wiki/A650_road" title="A650 road">A650</a> roads.
</p><p>The Council is working with <a href="/wiki/West_Yorkshire_Metro" title="West Yorkshire Metro">West Yorkshire Metro</a>, the other four West Yorkshire district councils and transport operators to provide an integrated transport system for the district through the implementation of the West Yorkshire <a href="/wiki/Local_Transport_Plan" class="mw-redirect" title="Local Transport Plan">Local Transport Plan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
A network of local buses, coordinated by West Yorkshire Metro and departing from <a href="/wiki/Wakefield_bus_station" title="Wakefield bus station">Wakefield bus station</a> in the town centre, serves Wakefield and district. Buses are operated by <a href="/wiki/Arriva_Yorkshire" title="Arriva Yorkshire">Arriva Yorkshire</a>, B L Travel, Poppletons, <a href="/wiki/Stagecoach_Yorkshire" title="Stagecoach Yorkshire">Stagecoach Yorkshire</a> and <a href="/wiki/National_Express_Coaches" title="National Express Coaches">National Express</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WBS_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WBS-87">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Wakefield_Kirkgate_railway_station" title="Wakefield Kirkgate railway station">Wakefield Kirkgate</a> was opened by the <a href="/wiki/Manchester_and_Leeds_Railway" title="Manchester and Leeds Railway">Manchester and Leeds Railway</a> in 1840. Wakefield Kirkgate is unstaffed and operated by <a href="/wiki/Northern_(train_operating_company)" title="Northern (train operating company)">Northern</a> who operate trains to <a href="/wiki/Barnsley_Interchange" title="Barnsley Interchange">Barnsley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meadowhall_Interchange" title="Meadowhall Interchange">Meadowhall Interchange</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sheffield_railway_station" class="mw-redirect" title="Sheffield railway station">Sheffield</a>, <a href="/wiki/Normanton_railway_station" title="Normanton railway station">Normanton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pontefract_Monkhill_railway_station" title="Pontefract Monkhill railway station">Pontefract</a>, <a href="/wiki/Knottingley_railway_station" title="Knottingley railway station">Knottingley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leeds_railway_station" title="Leeds railway station">Leeds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Castleford_railway_station" title="Castleford railway station">Castleford</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nottingham_railway_station" class="mw-redirect" title="Nottingham railway station">Nottingham</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> The station serves the <a href="/wiki/Hallam_Line" title="Hallam Line">Hallam Line</a>, <a href="/wiki/Huddersfield_Line" class="mw-redirect" title="Huddersfield Line">Huddersfield Line</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pontefract_Line" class="mw-redirect" title="Pontefract Line">Pontefract Line</a> of the MetroTrain network. <a href="/wiki/Grand_Central_(train_operating_company)" title="Grand Central (train operating company)">Grand Central</a> operating between <a href="/wiki/London_King%27s_Cross_railway_station" title="London King&#39;s Cross railway station">London King's Cross</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bradford_Interchange" title="Bradford Interchange">Bradford Interchange</a> stop at Kirkgate. In 2009 <a href="/wiki/CCTV" class="mw-redirect" title="CCTV">CCTV</a> was installed at the station, but it has acquired a reputation for being one of the country's worst stations.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> It is undergoing extensive renovation (2014).
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Wakefield_Westgate_railway_station" title="Wakefield Westgate railway station">Wakefield Westgate</a> opened in 1867 on the <a href="/wiki/Doncaster_railway_station" title="Doncaster railway station">Doncaster</a> to Leeds line. It has connections to the
<a href="/wiki/East_Coast_Main_Line" title="East Coast Main Line">East Coast Main Line</a>, trains to Leeds, Doncaster, and stations towards <a href="/wiki/London_King%27s_Cross_railway_station" title="London King&#39;s Cross railway station">London King's Cross</a>. <a href="/wiki/CrossCountry" title="CrossCountry">CrossCountry</a> trains go to <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_railway_station" title="Newcastle railway station">Newcastle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh_Waverley_railway_station" title="Edinburgh Waverley railway station">Edinburgh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Birmingham_New_Street_railway_station" title="Birmingham New Street railway station">Birmingham</a> and the <a href="/wiki/South_West_England" title="South West England">South West</a>. <a href="/wiki/East_Midlands_Railway" title="East Midlands Railway">East Midlands Railway</a> also run a limited service via Sheffield, <a href="/wiki/Derby_railway_station" title="Derby railway station">Derby</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leicester_railway_station" title="Leicester railway station">Leicester</a> to <a href="/wiki/St_Pancras_railway_station" title="St Pancras railway station">London St Pancras</a>. Wakefield Westgate is on the <a href="/wiki/Wakefield_Line" class="mw-redirect" title="Wakefield Line">Wakefield Line</a> of the <a href="/wiki/West_Yorkshire_Metro" title="West Yorkshire Metro">West Yorkshire Metro</a> network.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>  The line was <a href="/wiki/Rail_electrification" class="mw-redirect" title="Rail electrification">electrified</a> in 1989. Wakefield is served by inter-city express trains from both its railway stations. London can be reached in less than two hours.
</p><p>The nearest airport is <a href="/wiki/Leeds_Bradford_Airport" title="Leeds Bradford Airport">Leeds Bradford Airport</a>, 19 miles (31&#160;km) to the north of the city at <a href="/wiki/Yeadon,_West_Yorkshire" title="Yeadon, West Yorkshire">Yeadon</a>.
</p><p>The Aire and Calder Navigation is 33 miles (53&#160;km) from Leeds to <a href="/wiki/Goole" title="Goole">Goole</a>, and 7.5 miles (12.1&#160;km) from Wakefield to Castleford, and was created by Act of Parliament in 1699. It was opened to Leeds in 1704 and to Wakefield in 1706, enabling craft carrying 100 tons to reach Wakefield from the Humber.<sup id="cite_ref-Old_Wakefield_24-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Old_Wakefield-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> It is still used by a small amount of commercial traffic and leisure craft.<sup id="cite_ref-ACN_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ACN-91">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
The Calder and Hebble Navigation was created by Act of Parliament in 1758 with the intention of making the Calder navigable to <a href="/wiki/Sowerby_Bridge" title="Sowerby Bridge">Sowerby Bridge</a>. The route was originally surveyed by <a href="/wiki/John_Smeaton" title="John Smeaton">John Smeaton</a>, remains open and is used by leisure craft.<sup id="cite_ref-CHN_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHN-92">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> The Barnsley Canal, a broad canal with 20 locks, opened in 1799 connecting Barnsley to the Aire and Calder Navigation at Wakefield and was abandoned in 1953.<sup id="cite_ref-BC_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BC-93">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wakefield&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Old_Queen_Elizabeth_Grammar_School.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/The_Old_Queen_Elizabeth_Grammar_School.jpg/220px-The_Old_Queen_Elizabeth_Grammar_School.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/The_Old_Queen_Elizabeth_Grammar_School.jpg/330px-The_Old_Queen_Elizabeth_Grammar_School.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/The_Old_Queen_Elizabeth_Grammar_School.jpg/440px-The_Old_Queen_Elizabeth_Grammar_School.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="449" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Old_Queen_Elizabeth_Grammar_School.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The original <a href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_Grammar_School,_Wakefield" title="Queen Elizabeth Grammar School, Wakefield">Queen Elizabeth Grammar School</a> on Brook Street</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_schools_in_Wakefield" title="List of schools in Wakefield">List of schools in Wakefield</a></div>
<p>Wakefield's oldest surviving school is <a href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_Grammar_School,_Wakefield" title="Queen Elizabeth Grammar School, Wakefield">Queen Elizabeth Grammar School</a>, a boys-only school established in 1591 by <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Queen Elizabeth I</a> by Royal Charter.
The original building in Brook Street is now the 'Elizabethan Gallery'. QEGS moved to Northgate in 1854.<sup id="cite_ref-Historic_Wakefield_1066-1600_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Historic_Wakefield_1066-1600-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
The school was administered by the Governors of Wakefield Charities who opened Wakefield Girls High School <a href="/wiki/Wakefield_Girls%27_High_School" title="Wakefield Girls&#39; High School">WGHS</a> on Wentworth Street in 1878.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> These two schools today are <a href="/wiki/Independent_school" title="Independent school">independent schools</a>. <a href="/wiki/National_school_(England_and_Wales)" title="National school (England and Wales)">National schools</a> were opened by the Church of England including St Mary's in the 1840s and St John's in 1861.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> The original St Austin's Catholic School opened about 1838.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> A Methodist School was opened in Thornhill Street in 1846.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Pinders Primary School, originally Eastmoor School is the only school opened as a result of the Education Act 1870 which remains open today.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Wakefield_College" title="Wakefield College">Wakefield College</a> has its origins in the School of Art and Craft of 1868<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> and today is the major provider of <a href="/wiki/6th_form" class="mw-redirect" title="6th form">6th form</a> and <a href="/wiki/Further_education" title="Further education">further education</a> in the area, with around 3,000 full-time and 10,000 part-time students,<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> and campuses in the city and surrounding towns. In 2007 Wakefield City Council and Wakefield College announced plans to establish a University Centre of Wakefield but a bid for funding failed in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Other schools with sixth forms include: QEGS, Wakefield Girls High School, and Cathedral High School, which is now a <a href="/wiki/Performing_Arts_College" class="mw-redirect" title="Performing Arts College">Performing Arts College</a> for ages 11 to 18.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wakefield&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="wikitable floatright" style="margin-left: 1em; width: 30em; font-size: 90%;">
